                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ball+Milling&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ball%20milling">ball milling</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Closed+Pore&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_closed%20pore">closed pore</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Open+Pore&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_open%20pore">open pore</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Hard carbon is considered as a highly promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its beneficial characteristics, including abundant resources, low sodium storage potential, high capacity, and good cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Currently, various carbon sources, such as resins, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> biomass, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> and their derivatives like glucose, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> cellulose, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> and lignin, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> as well as pitch petroleum residue, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> are utilized to produce hard carbon. However, resin-derived hard carbon is criticized for its high cost, and petroleum and pitch-derived carbon materials exhibit low capacity (below 230 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), which hampers their commercial viability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 11 to 15">(11−15)</a> Biomass-derived hard carbon offers distinct advantages in terms of resource abundance, renewability, sustainability, and cost-effectiveness, making it appealing for SIBs. Previous studies have explored low-cost methods for preparing hard carbon by carbonizing biomass sources such as apricot shells, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> rice husks, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> banana peels, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> palm fruit calyx, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and cotton. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> However, the electrochemical performances of these materials are unsatisfactory since the carbon precursors are carbonized directly without an elaborate structural design or modification. Therefore, it is essential to engineer the structure of hard carbon materials to achieve superior electrochemical properties, thereby facilitating the commercialization of biomass-derived carbon for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The sodium-ion storage mechanism of hard carbon remains a subject of debate, posing challenges to enhancing its specific capacity and rate capability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> As is well-known, hard carbon typically consists of randomly oriented, curved, and defective graphene nanosheets, forming turbostratic structures with large interlayer distances. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Recent research by Hu et al. suggested that the sloping and plateau capacity is linked to the complex turbostratic structure and internal closed pore, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> However, there is still disagreement regarding the exact nature of sodium ions stored within the pores, with some studies observing metallic sodium and others only detecting sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> It is widely acknowledged that the pore structure of hard carbon significantly influences its electrochemical properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> In previous studies, we investigated the mechanism of formation of closed pores in hard carbon derived from wood and identified the factors that affect their formation. The as-prepared hard carbon with abundant closed pores exhibited a high reversible capacity of 430 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Therefore, it is believed that to achieve high-performance hard carbon, there is an urgent need to meticulously design the pore structure of hard carbon.</div><div class="NLM_p">Previous studies have shown several kinds of strategies to create the pore structure, thereby enhancing the electrochemical properties of hard carbon. For instance, Kamiyama et al. utilized MgO as a template to create nanosized pores in hard carbon materials, achieving a high reversible specific capacity of 478 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 25 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Similarly, Meng et al. employed ethanol as a pore agent to precisely control the closed-pore structure of hard carbon materials, obtaining a high sodium storage capacity of 410 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Despite these advancements, however, there is still a lack of efficient and scaleable strategies for tuning the pore structure, and the specific role of different types of pores (such as closed pores and open pores) in the sodium storage process has not been thoroughly investigated.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, the mechanochemical technique, which can induce physicochemical reactions under high-energy mechanical forces and regulate the carbon structure via introducing defects and heteroatoms, has attracted research attention. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> Lu et al. regulated the microstructure of the carbon layer in hard carbon via a ball milling approach to illustrate the sodium storage mechanism in hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Wang et al. proposed a dry ice-assisted ball milling process to increase the oxygen-containing functional groups in hard carbon, which demonstrates significantly improved specific capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> However, as their work demonstrates, ball milling hard carbon directly will significantly increase the specific surface area and introduce defect- and oxygen-containing functional groups, which will decrease the initial Coulombic efficiency of the hard carbon anode.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Based on the above analysis, biomass bamboo was chosen as the focus of our research due to its affordability, widespread availability, and high crystalline cellulose content. A straightforward ball milling pretreatment method was proposed to regulate the microstructure of hard carbon materials by adjusting the carbon chain of biomass precursors. Our results indicate that the shearing force generated during ball milling effectively breaks down the carbon chains in the bamboo precursor. The length of these carbon chains greatly influences the shrinkage and cross-linking behavior during the carbonization process, resulting in various pore structures ranging from closed to open pores. The optimized hard carbon with abundant closed pores exhibited a specific capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, significantly outperforming both bare hard carbon (243 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and hard carbon with abundant open pores (129 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Moreover, the kinetic analysis results further expand our understanding of the role of the pore structure in hard carbon materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Sample Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">The bamboo purchased from the Taobao store is 12–13 cm in diameter and about 2 m in length. The bamboo was divided into several sections, and the green skin on the surface was removed with a scraper. The bamboo powder was dried in a vacuum oven at 80 °C for 48 h and crushed in a small grinder for 10 min.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Twenty gram f bamboo powder and agate ball grinding beads was put into a 500 mL agate ball grinding tank in a ratio of 5:1. The ball grinding process was carried out by a planetary horizontal ball mill, the rotational speed was 500 rpm, and the ball milling time was set at 12 and 48 h, respectively. The bamboo precursors after ball milling time were named Balling-12h and Balling-48h. The hard carbon sample without ball milling was named Balling-0h. After carbonization at 1500 °C, the as-prepared hard carbon samples without ball milling and ball milling for 12 h were named Balling-0h-1500 and Balling-12h-1500, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The scanning electron microscope (SEM, JSM-7610FPlus) and the transmission electron microscope (TEM, FEI-Tian ETEM G2 80-300) were utilized to observe the microscopic morphology and structure. Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis was conducted by using a D/Max-III X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα (λ = 0.15406 nm) radiation at 30 kV and 30 mA. The structure was further examined through Raman spectra using a LabRAM JY-HR 800 spectrometer equipped with a 532 nm laser. Pore structure information was obtained via nitrogen adsorption/desorption by using a JWGB volumetric gas adsorption apparatus at 77 K. Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) was performed by using Xenocs Nano-InXider with a Cu Kα source. True density tests were carried out using an AccuPyc II 1340 instrument, with helium as the working gas. FTIR analysis was conducted by using a Thermos Nicolet iS50 FT-IR spectrophotometer in transmission mode with potassium bromide pellets. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo ESCALAB 250XI) was employed to investigate the surface chemical properties.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For the sodium-ion battery, electrochemical tests were conducted at room temperature using two-electrode coin cells (CR 2016). Pure Na foil was used as both the counter and the reference electrode. The working electrodes were prepared by mixing active materials, Super P, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) in a weight ratio of 8:1:1. This mixture was dissolved in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) and coated onto a copper foil, and the mass loading of active materials was kept more than 2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The electrolyte used was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethyl ether (DME). A glass fiber separator was employed in the sodium-ion battery. The assembly of the CR 2016 coin cell was performed inside an argon-filled glovebox with the content of O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O maintained below 1 ppm. Electrochemical tests were conducted using a Neware battery testing system, applying a voltage range of 0.01 to 2 V at room temperature. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were performed using a CHI760 electrochemical workstation.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The scanning electron microscope (SEM) images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a,f</a>) depict the distinct fiber structure of the original bamboo powder, with cross-section widths of approximately 100 μm. These fibers appeared smooth and densely packed. However, notable alterations in the fiber structure are observed after ball milling. After 12 h of ball milling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b,e</a>), the fibers exhibit fractures along with numerous fragments and small agglomerated particles. When further extending the ball milling time to 48 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c,f</a>), the long fibers are completely fragmented, forming large aggregates spanning from a few microns to hundreds of microns in size. These observations suggest that the shear force generated during ball milling effectively breaks down the long carbon chains of the bamboo precursor into shorter ones. And the carbon chain length gradually decreases as milling time increases.</div><div class="NLM_p">The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a) of bamboo powder samples before and after ball milling reveal three distinct diffraction peaks at 15°, 22°, and 35°, corresponding to the characteristic peaks of crystalline cellulose. Based on the Segal formula provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, the crystallinity of bamboo powder with different ball milling times (0, 12, and 48 h) was 48.6%, 39.2%, and 26.4%, respectively. This indicates that as the ball milling duration increases, the crystallinity of bamboo powder decreases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b), and the corresponding carbon chain length is shortened. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/medium/am4c08082_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns, (b) crystallinity degree, and (c) FTIR of bamboo precursors before and after balling. (d–f) XPS patterns of precursors with different ball milling durations.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c08082&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) analysis was conducted to examine the changes in the components of bamboo powder. The samples obtained with different ball milling durations exhibit characteristic peaks of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin. Specifically, the absorption peaks at 1510 and 1423 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the aromatic stretching vibration in the lignin molecule. The absorption peaks at 3428, 1160, and 2885 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to the strain O–H in hydroxy, antisymmetric C–O–C bridge stretching, and C–H stretching in cellulose. Additionally, the absorption peaks at 1730 and 1600 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the stretching vibration of C═O in hemicellulose (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). The presence of these characteristic peaks indicates that the types of functional groups in the precursor were not altered by the ball milling treatment.</div><div class="NLM_p">The characteristic spectra of C 1s in the X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d–f and in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. A comparison reveals that carbon atoms are primarily in the form of the C–C/C-H bond, C–O bond (286 eV in C 1s spectrum), and C═O bond (288.5 eV in C 1s spectrum). By analyzing the spectra, it is found that the proportion of C–C/C-H in the precursor after ball milling was significantly reduced, indicating that the carbon chains in the precursor were broken by the shear force. At the same time, the huge shearing physical action during the milling process will break the carbon chain, generating abundant free groups, which will combine with the oxygen or carbon dioxide in the ball mill tank, ultimately increasing the C–O content. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the aforementioned characterizations, it can be inferred that prolonged ball milling will further interrupt the original long carbon chain structure present in cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin, leading to the formation of shorter carbon chain structures.</div><div class="NLM_p">The calcination temperature of hard carbon was optimized, as demonstrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>; the hard carbon obtained at 1500 °C demonstrated the highest capacity. Therefore, the calcination temperature is set to 1500 °C. All the hard carbon samples prepared under different ball milling conditions display typical XRD patterns of hard carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a), showing two broad peaks corresponding to the (002) and (101) planes, characteristic of the amorphous structure of hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/medium/am4c08082_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman patterns, and (c) <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of as-prepared hard carbon. HRTEM images of (d) Balling-0h-1500, (e) Balling-12h-1500, and (f) Balling-48h-1500. (g) SAXS patterns, (h) closed-pore volume and the true density, and (i) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of as-prepared hard carbon.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c08082&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Raman spectroscopy is a valuable technique for characterizing the disordered structure of carbon materials. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, characteristic peaks of carbon materials are observed at 1340 cm<sup>–1</sup> (peak D), 1590 cm<sup>–1</sup> (peak G), 2680 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and 3000 cm<sup>–1</sup> (peak 2D). Peak D is induced by the defective structure in the hard carbon material, peak G is caused by the expansion vibration of the carbon mesh of sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid orbit in the carbon material, and peak 2D is attributed to the disordered structure of the carbon material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, with the increase of ball milling time, peak D of the final hard carbon product becomes higher and sharper and the strength of peak 2D gradually increases, which indicates that the disorder degree of the final hard carbon material is significantly improved with the extension of ball milling time. The intensity ratio of peak D to peak G, known as <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, can be used to assess the degree of defects in carbon materials. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values for Balling-0h-1500, Balling-12h-1500, and Balling-48h-1500 are 1.10, 1.23, and 1.37, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c), indicating changes in the orientation of the carbon layer and therefore pore structure formation in hard carbon materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of Balling-0h-1500, Balling-12h-1500, and Balling-48h-1500 reveal the significant impact of ball milling on the as-resulted carbon structure. The image of Balling-0h-1500 presents a typical amorphous carbon structure with closely arranged carbon layers along the <i>c</i>-axis direction. In contrast, Balling-12h-1500 exhibits abundant closed pores with a diameter of 2–3 nm, formed by the overlapping graphite-like layers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). Furthermore, Balling-48h-1500 demonstrates an obvious open pore structure with a pore diameter of 5–10 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f). The HRTEM results demonstrate the obvious change in pore structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further reveal the nature of the pore structure, the specific surface area parameters of the hard carbon samples were determined using the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption test. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i, Balling-0h-1500, Balling-12h-1500, and Balling-48h-1500 exhibit Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface areas of 7.16 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, 9.52 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, and 18.17 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. With the increase of ball milling time, the BET-specific surface area of the hard carbon samples will also increase. It is worth noting that the determination of specific surface areas by BET theory relies on the phenomenon of physical adsorption of gases on the external and internal surfaces of a porous material. However, since N<sub>2</sub> adsorption or desorption occurs primarily on the surface of carbon, it can only determine the “open pore” structure of carbon materials. Therefore, Balling-48h-1500 had the largest specific surface area.</div><div class="NLM_p">The pore structures in the hard carbon samples were further characterized by using small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) and a true density test. In SAXS analysis, the <i>Q</i>-value represents the scattering vector function with larger Q values indicating smaller pore and particle sizes. In the middle <i>Q</i>-value region (around 1 nm<sup>–1</sup>), the intensity evolution typically shows a plateau followed by a downward inflection point, indicating the presence of closed pores. In the SAXS analysis of all of the samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g), an obvious peak at approximately 1 nm<sup>–1</sup> is observed, indicating the presence of closed pores to some extent. Among these samples, Balling-12h-1500 exhibits the highest intensity hump, indicating the largest amount of closed pores, which is consistent with the findings from the TEM analysis. The results of the true density test (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h) confirm that Balling-12h-1500 had the largest number of closed pores, with a pore content of 0.261 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, consistent with the SAXS results. For Balling-48h-1500, an obvious open structure was observed, but the number of closed pores was small, indicating that closed pores transformed into open pores gradually.</div><div class="NLM_p">According to the above characterization, it can be inferred that with the increase of milling time, the pore structure transforms from closed pores with small volumes into open pores with larger volumes, suggesting that the ball milling pretreatment of bamboo powder can effectively tune the pore structure of the hard carbon material, thereby influencing its sodium storage capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">Combined with the previous characterization, the formation mechanism of the pore structure is speculated, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>. The shear force generated during ball milling effectively interrupts the carbon chain of the precursor. With the extension of milling time, the length of the carbon chain in the precursor gradually shortens. During subsequent carbonization, the carbon chain in the precursor experiences two main forces: mutual resistance (red arrow) and agglomeration contraction (blue arrow). In the case of the long carbon chain structure in the precursor without ball milling treatment, the carbon chains resist each other during carbonization, leading to the formation of a graphitized structure. After 12 h of ball milling, the length of the carbon chain is effectively shortened. During carbonization, both the carbon chains resist each other, and the shorter carbon chains tend to agglomerate. These two different behavior trends of carbon chains work together to form the closed-pore structure in the final carbon material. When the ball milling time is further extended to 48 h or more, the length of carbon chains in the precursor is further reduced. The resistance of excessively short carbon chains during the carbonization process becomes much less significant compared to the trend of agglomeration. As a result, the final carbon material forms an open pore structure with a larger volume.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/medium/am4c08082_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Schematic diagram of the influence of precursor carbon chain length on the closed-pore structure formation and sodium storage site.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c08082&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the sodium storage properties, constant current charge–discharge tests were conducted on the as-prepared hard carbon samples at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> within a potential window of 0.01–2.0 V. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, the sample without ball milling (Balling-0h-1500) exhibits a typical reversible capacity of 243 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with a plateau capacity of 183 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Conversely, the samples subjected to ball milling show significantly enhanced capacity. Particularly, Balling-12h-1500 demonstrates a reversible capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the highest plateau capacity of 262 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a,b</a>). This notable improvement in plateau capacity can be attributed to the abundant closed pores introduced by ball milling. However, it is noteworthy that when the ball milling time was extended to 48 h, both plateau capacity (71 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and total specific capacity (129 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) significantly decreased for Balling-48h-1500. This phenomenon may be associated with the different sodium-ion storage mechanisms of open pores compared with closed pores. According to reports, sodium ions are primarily adsorbed on the surface of open pores while filled in closed pores. Therefore, closed pores can accommodate more sodium ions. The rate capability testing demonstrated that Balling-12h-1500 exhibited the best rate capability with specific capacities of 356, 318, 298, 283, 269, and 258 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at different current densities of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). Moreover, Balling-12h-1500 also displayed good cycling performance with a retention ratio of 80% after 500 cycles at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). At a current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, Balling-12h-1500 demonstrated the capacity retention ratio of 74% and 77% after 500 cycles, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c</a>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/medium/am4c08082_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0004.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) The first galvanostatic discharge/charge profile, (b) summary of the capacity potential distribution, (c) rate capability, and (d) long-term cycling of Balling-0h-1500, Balling-12h-1500, and Balling-48h-1500.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c08082&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">According to the above discussion, the appropriate ball milling treatment can introduce abundant closed pores in carbon materials, enhancing the sodium-ion storage capacity, especially in terms of the platform capacity. This agrees well with the sodium-ion storage mechanism, in which the platform capacity is mainly attributed to the filling mechanism of sodium in the closed pores of hard carbon. However, excessive ball milling will promote the conversion of closed pores to open pores, decreasing sodium storage sites and specific capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further compare the kinetics of the samples, cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a–c) were utilized to accurately quantify the diffusive/capacitive contributions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d–f) and further elucidate the sodium-ion storage mechanism during the sodiation/desodiation processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> All samples exhibit similar shapes under various sweep rates from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. For the cathodic process, there is a main sharp peak at 0.01 V, which increases with the scan rate rising. The proportion of capacitive capacity contribution can be further quantified according to the following equation: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> <div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi mathvariant="italic">i</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi mathvariant="italic">V</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="italic">v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">v</mi></mrow><mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mo>/</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/medium/am4c08082_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0005.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. CV curves of Balling-0h-1500 (a), Balling-12h-1500 (b), and Balling-48h-1500 (c) under various sweep rates (from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>). Normalized contribution ratio of capacitive capacities of Balling-0h-1500 (d), Balling-12h-1500 (e), and Balling-48h-1500 (f) at different scan rates, (g) EIS spectra of Balling-12h-1500 and Balling-48h-1500, and (h) sodium-ion diffusion coefficient of Balling-12h-1500.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/asset/images/large/am4c08082_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c08082&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In this equation, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> correspond to the capacitive and diffusive behavior, respectively. By confirming <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>, the proportion of the contribution of the current at a specific potential can be quantified. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> From the normalized contribution ratio of capacitive capacities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d–f), the Balling-48h-1500 electrode demonstrates the highest capacitance contribution up to 96.17% at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a–c</a>), meaning the best dynamic kinetics. This is because the presence of a large specific surface area and excessive open pore structure enables the storage of sodium ions through the adsorption mechanism, thereby improving the dynamic kinetics of the hard carbon.</div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was further tested on these samples. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, the revealed charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of Balling-48h-1500 with an abundant open pore structure is only 0.75 Ω, while that of Balling-12h-1500 with an abundant closed-pore structure is 1.64 Ω. This is because the closed-pore structure can provide more filling sites for sodium ions, which will inevitably generate resistance in the embedding and removal processes of sodium ions, while the open pore can accommodate relatively fewer sodium ions via the adsorption mechanism. Similarly, the Warburg impedance (<i>R</i><sub>w</sub>) of the Balling-48h-1500 sample with an abundant open pore is only 1.65 Ω, smaller than that of the sample with an abundant closed-pore structure after 12h ball milling (<i>R</i><sub>w</sub> is 2.69 Ω), proving that the processes of adsorption and storage of sodium ions in open pores are smooth.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the above analysis, we carried out a galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) on Balling-12h-1500 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h) with a pulse current at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>(<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). According to Fick’s second law of diffusion, the diffusivity of sodium ion (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can be tested based on the following equation:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo></mrow><mrow><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>m</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">V</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">M</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi></mrow></mfrac></mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo></mrow><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>τ</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac></mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where τ is the pulse duration and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> are the hard carbon mass and molar mass of carbon, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is the molar volume, and <i>S</i> is the active surface area of the electrode. Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> can be obtained from the GITT curves. The results indicate that when the discharge voltage surpasses 0.1 V, the sodium-ion diffusion rate remains around 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting adsorption to surface and intralayer defects. Within the 0.1–0.05 V range, the diffusion coefficient gradually decreases due to enhanced repulsion between sodium ions from accumulation, corresponding to adsorption and interlayer intercalation. At the cutoff voltage (0.01 V), diffusion coefficients gradually rebound during the interlayer insertion/pore filling process, possibly because lower voltages reduce repulsion between ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> By comparing the electrochemical properties of the hard carbon materials with different pore structures, we found that the hard carbon materials (Balling-12h-1500) with more final closed pores showed a higher plateau capacity. Combined with the existing sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon, the plateau capacity mainly depends on the reversible embedding/exiting of sodium ions, and the slope capacity is mainly attributed to surface adsorption/desorption. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Therefore, we believe that the closed-pore structure can provide more embedding/exiting sites for sodium ions; thus, Balling-12h-1500 demonstrates high reversible capacity. However, the hard carbon material with more open pores (Balling-48h-1500) possesses better dynamic kinetics since the open pore structure can provide more surface adsorption/desorption sites for sodium ions. Despite the high pseudocapacitance, the capacity contribution from open pores is limited; therefore, Balling-48h-1500 demonstrates low capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In brief, we demonstrated the inconsistency between specific capacity and dynamic kinetics. We believe that the capacity increase brought by the closed-pore structure is through providing more accommodation sites for sodium ions, and the capacity brought about by these sites is reversible. As a result, hard carbon materials with rich closed-pore structures can have both high reversible specific capacity and high ICE. However, the dynamic kinetics on the plateau is significantly lower since the sodium ion needs to diffuse through the carbon layer before accommodation. The slow dynamic kinetics means high polarization at high rates, which will always lead to the deposition of sodium metal. Although the open pores can endow hard carbon with better dynamic kinetics, its contribution to capacity is low due to the limited surface. Therefore, to achieve hard carbon with high capability and excellent dynamic kinetics, the pore structure and the carbon layer that makes up the pores should be elaborately designed.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, hard carbon materials with different pore structures (closed and open pores) have been successfully obtained through a simple ball milling treatment on the bamboo precursor. It was demonstrated that the ball milling process effectively breaks down the long carbon chains of the precursor, thereby influencing the microstructure of the resulting hard carbon after carbonization. The type of pore structure demonstrated significant impact on the electrochemical properties. The hard carbon with an abundant closed-pore structure exhibited a notably enhanced capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> along with outstanding cycling stability. However, the kinetic analysis reveals that hard carbon with open pores possesses better sodium-ion diffusion kinetics than that with closed pores due to different sodium storage mechanisms. These results indicate that to obtain hard carbon simultaneously possessing high performance and excellent dynamic dynamics, it is necessary to carefully design the pore structure.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c08082?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c08082</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist1"><li><p class="inline">The SEM results of precursors and hard carbon materials with different ball milling times; the charge/discharge curves and long-term cycling performance of hard carbon samples prepared at different temperatures; the discharge/charge curves, long-term cycling performance, cyclic voltammograms, GITT potential profiles, XPS, and EIS of hard carbon materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c08082/suppl_file/am4c08082_si_001.pdf">am4c08082_si_001.pdf (0.99 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c08082" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">102-120</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.mtchem.2024.101903"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Z.                 10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.103937</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D64%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D103937%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DD.%2BSun%26author%3DB.%2BLuo%26author%3DH.%2BY.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BG.%2BTang%26author%3DX.%2BB.%2BJi%26author%3DL.%2BZ.%2BWang%26title%3DEngineering%2Bthe%2BTrap%2BEffect%2Bof%2BResidual%2BOxygen%2BAtoms%2Band%2BDefects%2Bin%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnode%2Btowards%2BHigh%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2019.103937&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c08082&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2019.103937&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering the trap effect of residual oxygen atoms and defects in hard carbon anode towards high initial Coulombic efficiency</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Dan; Luo, Bin; Wang, Haiyan; Tang, Yougen; Ji, Xiaobo; Wang, Lianzhou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">64</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">103937</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is a promising anode candidate for lithium/sodium ion batteries due to the key features of low operation potential and low cost, but its practical utilization is hindered by a challenging issue of poor initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which has not been understood well and resolved properly.  Herein, we report a new in-situ engineering approach to deliberately tune the residual oxygen atoms/defects of hard carbon by controlling the atm. of pyrolysis synthesis process and reveal important correlations between the ICE and residual oxygen atoms/defects.  When used as an anode in sodium ion battery, the hard carbon electrode with reduced residual oxygen atoms and defects can achieve a high av. ICE above 85%, which is considerably higher than the commonly obsd. ∼70% and ∼30% ICE values for pristine and acid treated hard carbon electrodes.  Encouragingly, a high reversible capacity of 310 mAh g-1 with good cycling stability (93% after 100 cycles) is demonstrated at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1.  The d. functional theory (DFT) calcn. and exptl. results reveal that the trap effects of residual oxygen atoms and defects on Na+ are the key factors that impact the ICE of the hard carbon electrode.  When the hard carbon is coupled with Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathode to form a sodium ion full cell, the battery delivers an impressively high energy d. of 239 Wh/kg (based on the active mass of anode and cathode without additives), which is among the best performing sodium ion full cells.  This work not only provides an effective approach to engineer the heteroatoms and defects in carbon-based materials but also sheds light on the design principle of practical hard carbon electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2018.06.036"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Beda, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Taberna, P. -L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Simon, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ghimbeu, C. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbons Derived from Green Phenolic Resins for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>139</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">248</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">257</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2018.06.036</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D139%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D248-257%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DA.%2BBeda%26author%3DP.%2B-L.%2BTaberna%26author%3DP.%2BSimon%26author%3DC.%2BM.%2BGhimbeu%26title%3DHard%2BCarbons%2BDerived%2Bfrom%2BGreen%2BPhenolic%2BResins%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2018.06.036&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c08082&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2018.06.036&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard carbons derived from green phenolic resins for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Beda, Adrian; Taberna, Pierre-Louis; Simon, Patrice; Matei Ghimbeu, Camelia</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">139</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">248-257</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have become recently one of the most promising classes of anode materials for Na ion batteries (NIBs) owing to their high specific capacity and good cycling stability.  Among the precursors used to prep. hard C, phenolic resins are of great interest due to their high C yield, however, their toxicity must be overcome.  The authors propose a green, simple and scalable procedure to obtain phenolic resins which by pyrolysis at high temp. (&gt;1000°) result in eco-friendly hard carbons with low surface area, disordered structure and high C yield.  The influence of several synthesis parameters (type of solvent, thermopolymn./annealing temp. and gas flow) was studied to det. the impact on both phenolic resin and hard C characteristics.  The synthesis time (12 h-3 days) depends on the used solvent whereas the C yield (25-35%) on the crosslinking degree which could be controlled by adjusting both thermopolymn. temp. and atm.  The structure of the hard carbons mainly changed with the carbonization temp. (1100-1700°) while the texture of the material was sensitive to most of the studied parameters.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing the closed pore formation of waste wood-derived hard carbon for advanced sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tang, Zheng; Zhang, Rui; Wang, Haiyan; Zhou, Siyu; Pan, Zhiyi; Huang, Yuancheng; Sun, Dan; Tang, Yougen; Ji, Xiaobo; Amine, Khalil; Shao, Minhua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the closed pore structure plays a key role in contributing low-voltage plateau capacity of hard carbon anode for sodium-ion batteries, the formation mechanism of closed pores is still under debate.  Here, we employ waste wood-derived hard carbon as a template to systematically establish the formation mechanisms of closed pores and their effect on sodium storage performance.  We find that the high crystallinity cellulose in nature wood decomps. to long-range carbon layers as the wall of closed pore, and the amorphous component can hinder the graphitization of carbon layer and induce the crispation of long-range carbon layers.  The optimized sample demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 430 mAh g-1 at 20 mA g-1 (plateau capacity of 293 mAh g-1 for the second cycle), as well as good rate and stable cycling performances (85.4% after 400 cycles at 500 mA g-1).                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The recent progress of pitch-based carbon anodes in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Mingchi; Sun, Ning; Ali Soomro, Razium; Xu, Bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Energy Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">55</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">34-47</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant attentions as promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale energy storage applications.  Here carbon materials are considered as the most competitive anodes for SIBs based on their low-cost, abundant availability and excellent structural stability.  Pitch, with high carbon content and low cost, is an ideal raw precursor to prep. carbon materials for large-scale applications.  Nevertheless, the microstructures of pitch-based carbon are highly ordered with smaller interlayer distances, which are unfavorable for Na ion storage.  Many efforts have been made to improve the sodium storage performance of pitch-based carbon materials.  This review summarizes the recent progress about the application of pitch-based carbons for SIBs anodes in the context of carbon's morphol. and structure regulation strategies, including morphol. adjustment, heteroatoms doping, fabricating heterostructures, and the increase of the degree of disorder.  Besides, the advantages, present challenges, and possible solns. to current issues in pitch-based carbon anode are discussed, with the highlight of future research directions.  This review will provide a deep insight into the development of low-cost and high-performance pitch-based carbon anode for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902852"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jensen, A.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Regulating Pore Structure of Hierarchical Porous Waste Cork-Derived Hard Carbon Anode for Enhanced Na Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yuqi; Lu, Yaxiang; Meng, Qingshi; Jensen, Anders C. S.; Zhang, Qiangqiang; Zhang, Qinghua; Tong, Yuxin; Qi, Yuruo; Gu, Lin; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous structure design is generally considered to be a reliable strategy to boost ion transport and provide active sites for disordered carbon anodes of Na-ion batteries (NIBs).  Herein, a type of waste cork-derived hard carbon material (CC) is reported for efficient Na storage via tuning the pore species.  Benefiting from the natural holey texture of this renewable precursor, CCs deliver a novel hierarchical porous structure.  The effective skeletal d. test combined with small angle X-ray scattering anal. (SAXS) is used to obtain the closed pore information.  Based on a detailed correlation anal. between pore information and the electrochem. performance of CCs, improving pyrolysis temp. to reduce open pores (related to initial capacity loss) and increase closed pores (related to plateau capacity) endows an optimal CC with a high specific capacity of ≈360 mAh g-1 in half-cells and a high energy d. of 230 Wh kg-1 in full-cells with a capacity retention of 71% after 2000 cycles at 2C rate.  The bioinspired high temp. pore-closing strategy and the new insights about the pore structure-performance relationship provide a rational guide for designing porous carbon anode of NIBs with tailored pore species and high Na storage capacity.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201900005"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y. -S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Slope-Dominated Carbon Anode with High Specific Capacity and Superior Rate Capability for High Safety Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Slope-Dominated Carbon Anode with High Specific Capacity and Superior Rate Capability for High Safety Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qi, Yuruo; Lu, Yaxiang; Ding, Feixiang; Zhang, Qiangqiang; Li, Hong; Huang, Xuejie; Chen, Liquan; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The comprehensive performance of carbon anodes for Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is largely restricted by their inferior rate capability and safety issues.  Herein, a slope-dominated carbon anode is achieved at a low temp. of 800°C, which delivers a high reversible capacity of 263 mA h g-1 at 0.15C with an impressive initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 80%.  When paired with the NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode, the reversible capacity at 6C is still 75% of that at 0.15C, and 73% of the capacity is retained after 1000 cycles at 3C.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Coal-based carbon anodes for high-performance potassium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Nan; Zhang, Xiaoyu; Liu, Chang; Wang, Yuwei; Li, Hongqiang; Qiu, Jieshan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) are considered as a viable alternative to lithium-ion batteries in large scale stationary energy storage.  However, the low cost, high rate and long life anode still remains great challenge for the commercialization of PIBs.  Herein, high performance carbon anodes for PIBs were successfully prepd. from low-cost and abundant coal through ball-milling, acid washing, oxidn. and carbonization.  We discover that the potassium storage performances of coal-based carbons are closely related to the coal rank.  A middle-rank bituminous coal-based carbon anode delivers a high reversible capacity of 260 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1 and maintains 118 mAh g-1 after 1200 cycles at 1 A g-1.  Moreover, at higher current densities of 0.1, 0.5, and 5.0 A g-1, it maintains high storage capacities of 230, 176, and 88 mA h g-1, resp.  The low cost and high performance coal-based carbon anodes are supposed to accelerate the development of PIBs toward future large-scale applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA08601A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Superior Low-Cost Amorphous Carbon Anode Made from Pitch and Lignin for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A superior low-cost amorphous carbon anode made from pitch and lignin for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yunming; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a promising candidate for grid electricity storage due to their potential low cost.  The development of anode materials is a crucial step to promote the commercialization of SIBs, and amorphous carbon materials are likely to be the most promising alternatives among all proposed anode materials.  However, the cost of the reported carbon materials is still very high due to the expensive precursors and their low carbon yield.  Here, we report an amorphous carbon (AC) material made from low cost pitch.  The amorphous carbon material with an amazing high carbon yield of 57% was achieved by utilizing the emulsification interaction between pitch and lignin to suppress the graphitization of pitch during the carbonization.  The effects of heat-treatment temps. and the pitch/lignin mass ratios on the morphol., microstructure and the electrochem. performance of AC were systematically investigated.  By optimizing exptl. conditions, we achieved one representative AC with a suitable morphol. and microstructure, which exhibits promising performances with a high reversible capacity of 254 mA h g-1, a high initial coulombic efficiency of 82% and excellent cycling stability.  This is the first demonstration that the pitch can be successfully applied in fabricating amorphous carbon anode materials for SIBs with superior low cost and high performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2017.12.103"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Porous Biomass-Derived Anode for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>129</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">695</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">701</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A porous biomass-derived anode for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Youyu; Chen, Mingming; Li, Qi; Yuan, Chao; Wang, Chengyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although hard carbons have been turned out to be suitable for sodium storage, their high cost and low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) need to be addressed for the com. application.  Here, we report an effective route including a pyrolysis process and a reductive strategy to synthesize high-performance hard carbons from waste apricot shell.  The obtained hard carbons inherit the unique architecture of the apricot shell, delivering a large interlayer spacing and a well-connected structure, which are beneficial for the Na+ intercalation and transport. The final prepd. hard carbons exhibit a promising anode performance with a desirable reversible capacity of ca. 400 mAhg-1, an improved ICE of 79% and an excellent cycling stability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn ever-increasing attention for scalable elec. energy storage owing to the inexhaustible sources and wide distribution of sodium.  However, to develop feasible anode materials still remains a great challenge for the practical application of SIBs.  Here, the authors report hard carbons derived from a plentiful and deserted biomass of rice husk through a facile acid treatment and subsequent pyrolysis.  The study illustrates that the electrochem. properties of the rice husk-derived hard carbons (RHHCs) are significantly influenced by the pyrolysis temp. because of the discrepancy in their microstructure.  The RHHC pyrolyzed at 1300° (RHHC-1300) shows the highest reversible capacity of 372 mAh/g and good cycling stability and rate performance due to its large interlayer distance and suitable oxygen content.  Also, full sodium-ion batteries are assembled to examine the application prospect using Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C and RHHC-1300 as cathode and anode materials, resp., delivering a high-energy d. of 185 Wh/kg and stable cycling performance.  This work could intensify the fundamental understanding of the sodium storage mechanism in biomass-derived hard carbons.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.5b05443"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Pengzi; Qiao, Bin; Du, Yichen; Li, Yafei; Zhou, Xiaosi; Dai, Zhihui; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

Here the authors report for the 1st time the application of F-doped C particles (F-CP) synthesized through pyrolysis of lotus petioles as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  Electrochem. tests demonstrate that the F-CP electrode delivers an initial charge capacity of 230 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 between 0.001 and 2.8 V, which greatly outperforms the corresponding value of 149 mA h g-1 for the counterpart banana peels-derived C (BPC).  Even under 200 mA g-1, the F-CP electrode could still exhibit a charge capacity of 228 mA h g-1 with initial charge capacity retention of 99.1% after 200 cycles compared to the BPC electrode with 107 mA h g-1 and 71.8%.  The F-doping and the large interlayer distance as well as the disorder structure contribute to a lowering of the Na ion insertion-extn. barrier, thus promoting the Na+ diffusion and providing more active sites for Na+ storage.  In specific, the F-CP electrode shows longer low-discharge-plateau and better kinetics than does the common C-based electrode.  The unique electrochem. performance of F-CP enriches the existing knowledge of the C-based electrode materials and broadens avenues for rational design of anode materials in Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2019.227008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Damodar, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ghosh, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rani, M. The authors report the synthesis of hard C anode materials from sepals of palm fruit calyx (bio-waste) that are prepd. by carbonization at moderate temp. between 500° and 900°.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Regulating closed pore structure enables significantly improved sodium storage for hard carbon pyrolyzing at relatively low temperature</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Siyu; Tang, Zheng; Pan, Zhiyi; Huang, Yuancheng; Zhao, Le; Zhang, Xi; Sun, Dan; Tang, Yougen; Dhmees, Abdelghaffar S.; Wang, Haiyan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">SusMat</span>
